</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Snow_roads_and_runways">Snow roads and runways</span></h4>
<p>Snow can be compacted to form a <a href="/wiki/Snow_road" title="Snow road">snow road</a> and be part of a <a href="/wiki/Winter_road" title="Winter road">winter road route</a> for vehicles to access isolated communities or construction projects during the winter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abele_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abele-70">[70]</a></sup> Snow can also be used to provide the supporting structure and surface for a runway, as with the <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_Airfield" title="Phoenix Airfield">Phoenix Airfield</a> in Antarctica. The snow-compacted runway is designed to withstand approximately 60 wheeled flights of heavy-lift military aircraft a year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-usap4212_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-usap4212-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture">Agriculture</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Indus.A2002274.0610.1km.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1700" data-file-width="1300" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Indus.A2002274.0610.1km.jpg/220px-Indus.A2002274.0610.1km.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Indus.A2002274.0610.1km.jpg/330px-Indus.A2002274.0610.1km.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Indus.A2002274.0610.1km.jpg/440px-Indus.A2002274.0610.1km.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Indus.A2002274.0610.1km.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Satellite view of the Indus River, showing snow in the Himalayas, which feeds it, and green areas that draw on it for irrigation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Snowfall can be beneficial to agriculture by serving as a <a href="/wiki/Thermal_insulation" title="Thermal insulation">thermal insulator</a>, conserving the heat of the Earth and protecting <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">crops</a> from subfreezing weather. Some agricultural areas depend on an accumulation of snow during winter that will melt gradually in spring, providing water for crop growth, both directly and via runoff through streams and rivers, which supply irrigation canals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snowenclyclopedia_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-Snowenclyclopedia-1">[1]</a></sup> The following are examples of rivers that rely on meltwater from glaciers or seasonal snowpack as an important part of their flow on which irrigation depends: the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a>, many of whose tributaries rise in the <a href="/wiki/Himalayas" title="Himalayas">Himalayas</a> and which provide much irrigation in northeast <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a>, which rises in <a href="/wiki/Tibet" title="Tibet">Tibet</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> and provides irrigation water to <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> from rapidly retreating Tibetan glaciers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Colorado_River" title="Colorado River">Colorado River</a>, which receives much of its water from seasonal snowpack in the <a href="/wiki/Rocky_Mountains" title="Rocky Mountains">Rocky Mountains</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-USGSrivers_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-USGSrivers-75">[75]</a></sup> and provides irrigation water to some 4 million acres (1.6 million hectares).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structures">Structures</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hoedhuette.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1203" data-file-width="1714" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Hoedhuette.jpg/220px-Hoedhuette.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Hoedhuette.jpg/330px-Hoedhuette.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Hoedhuette.jpg/440px-Hoedhuette.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hoedhuette.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Snow accumulation on building roofs</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Snow is an important consideration for loads on structures. To address these, European countries employ <i>Eurocode 1: Actions on structures - Part 1-3: General actions - Snow loads</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eurocode1_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eurocode1-77">[77]</a></sup> In North America, ASCE <i>Minimum Design Loads for Buildings and Other Structures</i> gives guidance on snow loads.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASCE7_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-ASCE7-78">[78]</a></sup> Both standards employ method that translate maximum expected ground snow loads onto design loads for roofs.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Roofs">Roofs</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ice_dam_and_roof_leakage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3206" data-file-width="2493" height="283" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Ice_dam_and_roof_leakage.jpg/220px-Ice_dam_and_roof_leakage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Ice_dam_and_roof_leakage.jpg/330px-Ice_dam_and_roof_leakage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Ice_dam_and_roof_leakage.jpg/440px-Ice_dam_and_roof_leakage.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ice_dam_and_roof_leakage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Icings resulting from water leaking through the roof due to an ice dam, caused by snowmelt on the roof.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Snow loads and icings are two principal issues for roofs. Snow loads are related to the climate in which a structure is sited. Icings are usually a result of the building or structure generating heat that melts the snow that is on it.</p>
<p><i>Snow loads</i> â The <i>Minimum Design Loads for Buildings and Other Structures</i> gives guidance on how to translate the following factors into roof snow loads:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ASCE7_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-ASCE7-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Ground snow loads</li>
<li>Exposure of the roof</li>
<li>Thermal properties of the roof</li>
<li>Shape of the roof</li>
<li>Drifting</li>
<li>Importance of the building</li>
</ul>
<p>It gives tables for ground snow loads by region and a methodology for computing ground snow loads that may vary with elevation from nearby, measured values. The <i>Eurocode 1</i> uses similar methodologies, starting with ground snow loads that are tabulated for portions of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eurocode1_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eurocode1-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Icings</i> â Roofs must also be designed to avoid <a href="/wiki/Ice_dam_(roof)" title="Ice dam (roof)">ice dams</a>, which result from meltwater running under the snow on the roof and freezing at the eave. Ice dams on roofs form when accumulated snow on a sloping roof melts and flows down the roof, under the insulating blanket of snow, until it reaches below freezing temperature air, typically at the <a href="/wiki/Eaves" title="Eaves">eaves</a>. When the meltwater reaches the freezing air, ice accumulates, forming a dam, and snow that melts later cannot drain properly through the dam.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Paul_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-Paul-79">[79]</a></sup> Ice dams may result in <a href="/wiki/Water_damage" title="Water damage">damaged</a> building materials or in damage or injury when the ice dam falls off or from attempts to remove ice dams. The melting results from heat passing through the roof under the highly insulating layer of snow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hansen_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hansen-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Utility_lines">Utility lines</span></h4>
<p>In areas with trees, utility distribution lines on poles are less susceptible to snow loads than they are subject to damage from trees falling on them, felled by heavy, wet snow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Elsewhere, snow can accrete on power lines as "sleeves" of rime ice. Engineers design for such loads, which are measured in kg/m (lb/ft) and power companies have forecasting systems that anticipate types of weather that may cause such accretions. Rime ice may be removed manually or by creating a sufficient short circuit in the affected segment of power lines to melt the accretions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports_and_recreation">Sports and recreation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skier-carving-a-turn.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2187" data-file-width="3331" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Skier-carving-a-turn.jpg/220px-Skier-carving-a-turn.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Skier-carving-a-turn.jpg/330px-Skier-carving-a-turn.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Skier-carving-a-turn.jpg/440px-Skier-carving-a-turn.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skier-carving-a-turn.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Alpine skier carving a turn.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Winter_sport" title="Winter sport">Winter sport</a></div>
<p>Snow figures into many winter sports and forms of recreation, including <a href="/wiki/Skiing" title="Skiing">skiing</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sledding" title="Sledding">sledding</a>. Common examples include <a href="/wiki/Cross-country_skiing" title="Cross-country skiing">cross-country skiing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alpine_skiing" title="Alpine skiing">Alpine skiing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snowboarding" title="Snowboarding">snowboarding</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snow_shoe" title="Snow shoe">snow shoeing</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Snowmobile" title="Snowmobile">snowmobiling</a>. The design of the equipment used, typically relies on the bearing strength of snow, as with skis or snowboards and contends with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coefficient_of_friction" title="Coefficient of friction">coefficient of friction</a> of snow to allow sliding, often enhance by <a href="/wiki/Ski_wax" title="Ski wax">ski waxes</a>.</p>
<p>By far the largest form of winter recreation is skiing. As of 1994, of the estimated 65â75 million skiers worldwide, there were approximately 55 million who engaged in <a href="/wiki/Alpine_skiing" title="Alpine skiing">Alpine skiing</a>, the rest engaged in <a href="/wiki/Cross-country_skiing" title="Cross-country skiing">cross-country skiing</a>. Approximately 30 million skiers (of all kinds) were in Europe, 15 million in the US, and 14 million in Japan. As of 1996, there were reportedly 4,500 ski areas, operating 26,000 ski lifts and enjoying 390 million skier visits per year. The preponderant region for downhill skiing was Europe, followed by Japan and the US.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></p>
<p>Increasingly, ski resorts are relying on <a href="/wiki/Snowmaking" title="Snowmaking">snowmaking</a>, the production of snow by forcing water and pressurized air through a <a href="/wiki/Snowmaking#Snowmaking_guns" title="Snowmaking">snow gun</a> on ski slopes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Snowmaking is mainly used to supplement natural snow at <a href="/wiki/Ski_resort" title="Ski resort">ski resorts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> This allows them to improve the reliability of their snow cover and to extend their ski seasons from late autumn to early spring. The production of snow requires low temperatures. The threshold temperature for snowmaking increases as humidity decreases. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wet_bulb_temperature" title="Wet bulb temperature">Wet bulb temperature</a> is used as a metric since it takes air temperature and relative humidity into account. Snowmaking is a relatively expensive process in its energy consumption, thereby limiting its use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ski_wax" title="Ski wax">Ski wax</a> enhances the ability of a ski or other runner to slide over snow, which depends on both the properties of the snow and the ski to result in an optimum amount of lubrication from melting the snow by friction with the skiâtoo little and the ski interacts with solid snow crystals, too much and capillary attraction of meltwater retards the ski. Before a ski can slide, it must overcome the maximum value static friction. Kinetic (or dynamic) friction occurs when the ski is moving over the snow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhavikatti_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bhavikatti-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cold-weather_warfare" title="Cold-weather warfare">Cold-weather warfare</a></div>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ski_warfare" title="Ski warfare">Ski warfare</a></div>
<p>Snow affects warfare conducted in winter, alpine environments or at high latitudes. The main factors are <i>impaired visibility</i> for acquiring targets during falling snow, <i>enhanced visibility</i> of targets against snowy backgrounds for targeting, and mobility for both <a href="/wiki/Mechanized_infantry" title="Mechanized infantry">mechanized</a> and <a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">infantry</a> troops. Snowfall can severely inhibit the <a href="/wiki/Military_logistics" title="Military logistics">logistics of supplying troops</a>, as well. Snow can also provide cover and fortification against small-arms fire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-chew_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-chew-90">[90]</a></sup> Noted <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Winter_warfare" title="Winter warfare">winter warfare</a> campaigns where snow and other factors affected the operations include:</p>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia" title="French invasion of Russia">French invasion of Russia</a>, where poor traction conditions for ill-shod horses made it difficult for supply wagons to keep up with troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> That campaign was also strongly affected by cold, whereby the retreating army reached <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neman_River" title="Neman River">Neman River</a> in December 1812 with only 10,000 of the 420,000 that had set out to invade <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a> in June of the same year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Wordsworth_Pocket_Encyclopedia_p._17_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Wordsworth_Pocket_Encyclopedia_p._17-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Winter_War" title="Winter War">Winter War</a>, an attempt by the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> to take territory in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> in late 1939 demonstrated superior winter tactics of the <a href="/wiki/Finnish_Army" title="Finnish Army">Finnish Army</a>, regarding over-snow mobility, <a href="/wiki/Camouflage" title="Camouflage">camouflage</a>, and use of the terrain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_the_Bulge" title="Battle of the Bulge">Battle of the Bulge</a>, a German counteroffensive during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, starting December 16, 1944, was marked by heavy snowstorms that hampered allied air support for ground troops, but also impaired German attempts to supply their front lines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> On the Eastern Front with the Nazi invasion of Russia in 1941, <a href="/wiki/Operation_Barbarossa" title="Operation Barbarossa">Operation Barbarossa</a>, both Russian and German soldiers had to endure terrible conditions during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_winter" title="Russian winter">Russian winter</a>. While use of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ski_infantry" title="Ski infantry">ski infantry</a> was common in the Red Army, Germany formed only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/1st_Ski_Division_(Germany)" title="1st Ski Division (Germany)">one division</a> for movement on skis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Clemmesen_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-Clemmesen-95">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> which lasted from June 25, 1950, until an armistice in July 27, 1953, began when <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korea</a> invaded <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korea</a>. Much of the fighting occurred during winter conditions, involving snow,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> notably during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Chosin_Reservoir" title="Battle of Chosin Reservoir">Battle of Chosin Reservoir</a>, which was a stark example of cold affecting military operations,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> especially vehicles and weapons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<center>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Effects_on_ecosystems">Effects on ecosystems</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chlamydomonas_nivalis.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Chlamydomonas_nivalis.jpg/220px-Chlamydomonas_nivalis.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Chlamydomonas_nivalis.jpg/330px-Chlamydomonas_nivalis.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Chlamydomonas_nivalis.jpg/440px-Chlamydomonas_nivalis.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chlamydomonas_nivalis.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Algae, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlamydomonas_nivalis" title="Chlamydomonas nivalis">Chlamydomonas nivalis</a>, that thrive in snow form red areas in the <a href="/wiki/Suncup_(snow)" title="Suncup (snow)">suncups</a> on this snow surface.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Both plant and animal life endemic to snow-bound areas develop ways to adapt. Among the adaptive mechanisms for plants are dormancy, seasonal dieback, survival of seeds; and for animals are hibernation, insulation, anti-freeze chemistry, storing food, drawing on reserves from within the body, and clustering for mutual heat.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SnowEcology_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-SnowEcology-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plant_life">Plant life</span></h3>
<p>Snow interacts with vegetation in two principal ways, vegetation can influence the deposition and retention of snow and, conversely, the presence of snow can affect the distribution and growth of vegetation. Tree branches, especially of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conifer" title="Conifer">conifers</a> intercept falling snow and prevent accumulation on the ground. Snow suspended in trees ablates more rapidly than that on the ground, owing to its greater exposure to sun and air movement. Trees and other plants can also promote snow retention on the ground, which would otherwise be blown elsewhere or melted by the sun. Snow affects vegetation in several ways, the presence of stored water can promote growth, yet the annual onset of growth is dependent on the departure of the snowpack for those plants that are buried beneath it. Furthermore, avalanches and erosion from snowmelt can scour terrain of vegetation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Snowenclyclopedia_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-Snowenclyclopedia-1">[1]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animal_life">Animal life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fjellrev_-_Arctic_fox_(24490250823).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Fjellrev_-_Arctic_fox_%2824490250823%29.jpg/220px-Fjellrev_-_Arctic_fox_%2824490250823%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Fjellrev_-_Arctic_fox_%2824490250823%29.jpg/330px-Fjellrev_-_Arctic_fox_%2824490250823%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fa/Fjellrev_-_Arctic_fox_%2824490250823%29.jpg/440px-Fjellrev_-_Arctic_fox_%2824490250823%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fjellrev_-_Arctic_fox_(24490250823).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Arctic_fox" title="Arctic fox">Arctic fox</a>, a predator of smaller animals that live beneath the snow</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Snow supports a wide variety of animals both on the surface and beneath. Many <a href="/wiki/Invertebrate" title="Invertebrate">invertebrates</a> thrive in snow, including <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">spiders</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wasp" title="Wasp">wasps</a>, <a href="/wiki/Beetle" title="Beetle">beetles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Snow_scorpionfly" title="Snow scorpionfly">snow scorpionflys</a> and <a href="/wiki/Springtail" title="Springtail">springtails</a>. Such <a href="/wiki/Arthropod" title="Arthropod">arthropods</a> are typically active at temperatures down to â5Â Â°C (23Â Â°F). Invertebrates fall into two groups, regarding surviving subfreezing temperatures: freezing resistant and those that avoid freezing because they are freeze-sensitive. The first group may be cold hardy owing to the ability to produce <a href="/wiki/Antifreeze" title="Antifreeze">antifreeze</a> agents in their body fluids that allows survival of long exposure to sub-freezing conditions. Some organisms <a href="/wiki/Fasting" title="Fasting">fast</a> during the winter, which expels freezing-sensitive contents from their digestive tracts. The ability to survive the absence of oxygen in ice is an additional survival mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SnowEcology_99-1"><a href="#cite_note-SnowEcology-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>Small <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vertebrates" title="Vertebrates">vertebrates</a> are active beneath the snow. Among vertebrates, <a href="/wiki/Alpine_salamander" title="Alpine salamander">alpine salamanders</a> are active in snow at temperatures as low as â8Â Â°C (18Â Â°F); they burrow to the surface in springtime and lay their eggs in melt ponds. Among mammals, those that remain active are typically smaller than 250 grams (8.8Â oz). <a href="/wiki/Omnivore" title="Omnivore">Omnivores</a> are more likely to enter a torpor or be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hibernate" title="Hibernate">hibernators</a>, whereas <a href="/wiki/Herbivore" title="Herbivore">herbivores</a> are more likely to maintain food caches beneath the snow. <a href="/wiki/Vole" title="Vole">Voles</a> store up to 3 kilograms (6.6Â lb) of food and <a href="/wiki/Pika" title="Pika">pikas</a> up to 20 kilograms (44Â lb). Voles also huddle in communal nests to benefit from one another's warmth. On the surface, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wolves" title="Wolves">wolves</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coyotes" title="Coyotes">coyotes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fox" title="Fox">foxes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lynx" title="Lynx">lynx</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Weasel" title="Weasel">weasels</a> rely on these subsurface dwellers for food and often dive into the snowpack to find them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SnowEcology_99-2"><a href="#cite_note-SnowEcology-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Extraterrestrial_snow">Extraterrestrial snow</span></h2>
<p>Extraterrestrial "snow" includes water-based precipitation, but also precipitation of other compounds prevalent on other planets and moons in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a>. Examples are:</p>
<ul>
<li>On <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>, observations of the <a href="/wiki/Phoenix_(spacecraft)" title="Phoenix (spacecraft)"><i>Phoenix</i> Mars lander</a> reveal that water-based snow crystals occur at high latitudes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Additionally, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> precipitates from clouds during the Martian winters at the poles and contributes to a seasonal deposit of that compound, which is the principal component of that <a href="/wiki/Martian_polar_ice_caps" title="Martian polar ice caps">planet's ice caps</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></li>
<li>On <a href="/wiki/Venus" title="Venus">Venus</a>, observations from the <a href="/wiki/Magellan_(spacecraft)" title="Magellan (spacecraft)"><i>Magellan</i> spacecraft</a> reveal the presence a metallic substance, which precipitates as "<a href="/wiki/Venus_snow" title="Venus snow">Venus snow</a>" and leaves a highly reflective substance at the tops of Venus's highest mountain peaks resembling terrestrial snow. Given the high temperatures on Venus, the leading candidates for the precipitate are <a href="/wiki/Lead_sulfide" title="Lead sulfide">lead sulfide</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bismuth_sulfide" title="Bismuth sulfide">bismuth sulfide</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></li>
<li>On <a href="/wiki/Saturn" title="Saturn">Saturn</a>'s moon, <a href="/wiki/Titan_(moon)" title="Titan (moon)">Titan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cassini%E2%80%93Huygens" title="CassiniâHuygens"><i>CassiniâHuygens</i> spacecraft</a> observations suggest the presence of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a> or some other form of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a>-based crystalline deposits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
